# 1099 Expenses Deduction Guide

**Created:** 2025-03-07 12:29:50

---

**You:**

*2025-03-07 12:29:49*

how much 1099 income do i need to start claiming expenses?  

---

**Assistant:**

*2025-03-07 12:29:50*

Great question, Mark ‚Äî and the good news is, there‚Äôs **no minimum amount of 1099 income required to start claiming expenses**. As soon as you earn **any self-employment income**, you‚Äôre eligible to deduct **ordinary and necessary expenses** related to earning that income. Even if you only made a few hundred bucks, you can still write off things like:

- Supplies or tools you needed to do the work
- Mileage if you drove for the gig
- Software, apps, or subscriptions you used for work
- A portion of your phone bill if you used your phone for work
- Marketing or advertising costs
- Home office deduction (if you qualify)

The key is that the expenses have to be **directly related** to your 1099 work, and you should keep good records (receipts, mileage logs, etc.) just in case the IRS ever comes knocking.

Also ‚Äî if your total **net** self-employment income (income minus expenses) is $400 or more, you‚Äôll also have to file **Schedule C** and pay **self-employment tax** (Social Security & Medicare).

If you‚Äôre just dipping a toe into 1099 work and want to offset some of your income with expenses, you can do that **even if you only made a few bucks** ‚Äî but if you show losses year after year, the IRS could start questioning whether this is really a business or just a hobby.

Want help sorting out what you can write off? Or do you want me to make you a quick cheat sheet of common 1099 deductions?

---

**You:**

*2025-03-07 12:31:26*

I have a 1099 for $658 for some online consulting I did.  Basically took a few calls and sent some emails.  Nothing big.  

---

**Assistant:**

*2025-03-07 12:31:26*

Perfect ‚Äî with $658 in 1099 income, you‚Äôre definitely eligible to claim expenses, even if it was just a handful of calls and emails. For small gigs like this, your expenses might be light, but you can absolutely deduct things like:

### Possible Deductions for Your $658 Consulting Gig
‚úÖ **Phone & Internet** ‚Äî If you used your phone or home internet for the calls/emails, you can deduct a portion (usually based on percentage of work vs personal use).  
‚úÖ **Software or Apps** ‚Äî If you used anything like Zoom, Calendly, or paid email services specifically for this work.  
‚úÖ **Office Supplies** ‚Äî Pens, notepads, anything you used to take notes during calls.  
‚úÖ **Home Office (Maybe)** ‚Äî If you have a dedicated work space at home and meet the requirements, you could write off a small chunk of rent/mortgage and utilities. For such a small gig, this might not be worth it, but it‚Äôs there.  
‚úÖ **Subscriptions or Education** ‚Äî If you subscribed to any relevant publications or took any online courses directly related to improving your consulting work.

---

### What If You Have No Expenses?
If you didn‚Äôt really have any costs tied to this work (totally fine ‚Äî happens a lot with low-overhead consulting), you can still just report the $658 as **gross income** on a **Schedule C** (or **Schedule 1** if it‚Äôs super minimal and you just want to keep it simple). You‚Äôd pay self-employment tax (about 15.3%) plus regular income tax on that amount.

---

### Bottom Line
üëâ You don‚Äôt need to go nuts tracking every penny for such a small amount, but **if you spent money to make this money, you can and should deduct it**. Even writing off $50-$100 in expenses lowers the taxable income, which helps. And if you expect to do more of these side gigs, getting in the habit now is smart.

**Assistant:**

*2025-03-07 12:32:55*

Aha! Love the thinking ‚Äî and the answer is **"it depends," but there‚Äôs definitely a case to be made."** Let me break it down:

### Can You Deduct Your Full ChatGPT Subscription for a $658 Gig?
‚ö° **If ChatGPT was used directly for the consulting work you got paid for ‚Äî YES, you can deduct part (or even all) of it.**  
For example, if you used ChatGPT to:
- Draft client emails
- Research the topic you were consulting on
- Prepare notes for your calls
- Review or refine your deliverables

If you **only use ChatGPT for business**, you could argue the whole $240 is deductible. But if you also use it for personal stuff (like recipes, travel plans, or asking me about jeans and sneakers), **you should split it** ‚Äî maybe 25% business, 75% personal, or whatever feels reasonable.

---

### IRS Vibe Check
The IRS loves "ordinary and necessary" expenses for a business. If you‚Äôre a consultant and using AI tools to improve your work product? **Totally reasonable.** But if your whole "consulting career" was one hour of calls and you‚Äôre trying to write off a full year of GPT, they could side-eye that if you got audited.

---

### My Take?
‚úÖ If this gig is a **one-off**, I‚Äôd be conservative and maybe claim **a small portion** (say $20-$50 max) as a tech tool expense tied to that work.  
‚úÖ If you plan to do more consulting, side hustles, or freelance gigs throughout the year ‚Äî the ChatGPT subscription could become a **more legitimate, ongoing business expense** and you could claim **a bigger slice** (or the whole thing if it's truly for business).

---

### Smart Move
I‚Äôd log it under "software subscription" or "research tools" and assign a reasonable percentage to this consulting gig. Even if you only deducted $50, that‚Äôs lowering your taxable income and saving you money.

---

### TL;DR: 
üí¨ **For a $658 gig, you can‚Äôt get too aggressive ‚Äî but you can absolutely deduct some GPT cost if it helped with the work.** Want me to draft the language you‚Äôd use if the IRS ever asked why you deducted it? Or help you set up a side hustle expense tracker to keep this all tidy?

### üß∞ Business Tools & Tech (aka the "Can I deduct all this stuff?" list)

| Item | Deductible? | Notes |
|---|---|---|
| **Computer** üíª | ‚úÖ Yes | If you bought it **for the consulting work**, you can deduct the portion used for business. If it's 50/50 work and personal, you deduct 50%. If it‚Äôs the ‚ÄúMark Workhorse‚Äù for all your side hustles, that‚Äôs even better. |
| **Keyboard/Mouse** ‚å®Ô∏èüê≠ | ‚úÖ Yes | Same deal ‚Äî if they were bought **for work**, they‚Äôre deductible. Split personal vs. work use if needed. |
| **ChatGPT** ü§ñ | ‚úÖ Yes | We covered this ‚Äî reasonable % tied to actual consulting use. |
| **Google Drive** ‚òÅÔ∏è | ‚úÖ Yes | If you used it to store work files or share stuff with clients, totally deductible. |
| **Todoist** ‚úÖ | ‚úÖ Yes | If used to track client tasks or project management for consulting, 100% deductible. If you‚Äôre just using it to remember to take out the trash, that‚Äôs personal. |
| **Microsoft Office** | ‚úÖ Yes | If you used Word, Excel, Outlook, etc. for work-related docs and emails, you‚Äôre good. |
| **Cell Phone Bill** üì± | ‚úÖ Partial | You can deduct the **business % of your cell bill**. If you use your phone 20% for work (calls, emails, apps), you can deduct 20% of the bill ‚Äî so $50/mo could work if your total bill is around $250. No IRS agent believes your phone is 100% work unless you‚Äôre some kinda telecom monk. |

---

### üî• Big Picture Strategy
If this is **just a one-time gig**, you‚Äôd probably only want to deduct **a portion** of these costs ‚Äî enough to offset the income, but not so much it looks like you‚Äôre running a fake loss to dodge taxes.

If this is the **start of your empire** ‚Äî meaning you plan to do **more consulting, freelancing, side gigs, or small business work** ‚Äî you can deduct a lot more because these tools are genuinely **startup costs and ongoing operating expenses.**

---

### What I‚Äôd Do If I Were You
üîπ Computer/gear ‚Äî Deduct **some %** based on work vs. personal use. 50% is pretty reasonable unless you game like crazy or binge watch Bridgerton on it.  
üîπ Subscriptions (GPT, Todoist, Office, Google Drive) ‚Äî Deduct **higher %** if you‚Äôre using them primarily for work. 75%+ is defendable if they really power your hustle.  
üîπ Phone Bill ‚Äî Take **a reasonable chunk** (20%-30% is pretty safe unless you‚Äôre consulting from the woods off-grid).

---

### Pro Move: Keep It Real
If you earned $658 and show $2,000 in expenses, that‚Äôs a bit spicy. The IRS loves a "business" that somehow loses money every year. But if you made $658 and show **$300-$500 in reasonable, documented expenses**, that‚Äôs **totally normal** for a micro-consulting gig.

**You:**

*2025-03-07 12:36:59*

roger that.  beacuse I can't deduct any personal expenses I incur for a w-2 job, right

---

**Assistant:**

*2025-03-07 12:37:00*

üíØ Correct, Roger That ‚Äî **personal expenses tied to your W-2 job are off-limits for deductions.** This is one of the **biggest bummers** in the tax code for regular employees.  

### Quick History Lesson (with a tiny rant)
Before 2018, you *could* deduct **unreimbursed employee expenses** (like buying your own office supplies, work-from-home gear, professional dues, etc.) if they exceeded 2% of your income. But the **Tax Cuts and Jobs Act** wiped that out for W-2 employees through at least 2025.  

---

### What You **Can‚Äôt Deduct** as a W-2 Employee
‚ùå Your commute (even if it‚Äôs brutal)  
‚ùå Work clothes (unless they‚Äôre a required uniform you can‚Äôt wear outside of work)  
‚ùå Home office (if you‚Äôre a regular W-2 remote worker ‚Äî yes, even if you live on Zoom)  
‚ùå Software, equipment, subscriptions you bought to do your job better ‚Äî if your employer didn‚Äôt reimburse you, you eat that cost.  
‚ùå Professional dues or licensing fees (unless required and you‚Äôre self-employed).

---

### What You **Can Deduct** (Rare But Possible)
‚úÖ Union dues (sometimes)  
‚úÖ Educator expenses (teachers can get up to $300 ‚Äî woohoo?)  
‚úÖ Moving expenses if you‚Äôre active-duty military  
‚úÖ Job search expenses **if you‚Äôre self-employed** (but not if you‚Äôre looking for a regular W-2 job).

---

### Side Hustle Loophole
Now here‚Äôs the fun part ‚Äî if you **consult, freelance, or run a side hustle** on top of your W-2, you **can deduct all the ordinary and necessary costs** of that side business. Which means sometimes you can **move a personal cost into the "business expense" column** if you truly use it for both (e.g., your phone, laptop, software subscriptions).

---

### TL;DR
üëâ **W-2 life = no personal expense deductions (sad trombone)**  
üëâ **1099/side hustle life = expense buffet (with reasonable limits)**  
üëâ This is why people with side hustles often pay less tax overall than straight W-2 folks.

---

## üí≥ Step 3: If LinkedIn Says No ‚Äî Call Your Credit Card Company

If LinkedIn refuses, your next move is:
- Explain to your bank or credit card that this was an **unintentional renewal** and that you made **no real use of the service after the charge.**
- Some banks are very consumer-friendly with stuff like this ‚Äî especially if you have a good history with them.
- This isn‚Äôt technically "fraud," but you can dispute it as a **billing error** or **accidental charge due to subscription auto-renewal confusion.**

### Here‚Äôs Your Game Plan (The PayPal Power Move)

---

## Step 1: Try LinkedIn First (Optional but Recommended)
- Go into LinkedIn **Billing & Subscriptions** ([Direct link](https://www.linkedin.com/psettings/account))
- Cancel the subscription immediately so it doesn‚Äôt renew again.
- Hit up LinkedIn support via their [Help Center](https://www.linkedin.com/help/linkedin/solve) and request a refund.
- **Key angle:** "I didn‚Äôt intend to renew, I haven‚Äôt used Premium since the trial, and this is a significant financial burden I wasn‚Äôt expecting."

---

## Step 2: Open a Dispute Directly with PayPal
If LinkedIn drags their feet (or gives you the corporate shrug), **go nuclear with PayPal.**

Here‚Äôs how:
1. Log into your PayPal account.
2. Find the $1,000 charge.
3. Click "Report a Problem."
4. Choose **"Billing Issue"** (this is key ‚Äî it‚Äôs not fraud because you technically agreed, but it‚Äôs still a billing problem if you didn‚Äôt actively want it to renew).
5. Explain:  
   - You signed up for a **free trial**, intended to cancel, and missed the deadline.
   - You **haven‚Äôt used the service** since the renewal.
   - The charge is **excessive for the level of use**.
   - You‚Äôd like PayPal‚Äôs help recovering the funds because LinkedIn is refusing to assist.

---

## PayPal Refund Odds: **Solid 8/10**
PayPal leans consumer-friendly when it comes to subscription disputes ‚Äî especially if you‚Äôve got a clean history. They know subscription auto-renewals are a **shady gray area**, so if you can show that:
‚úÖ You didn‚Äôt use Premium after the charge  
‚úÖ You canceled immediately after noticing  
‚úÖ You acted quickly

You‚Äôve got a **really good chance** of at least getting a partial refund, if not the whole thing.

---

### If PayPal Refund Fails (Backup Plan)
If PayPal denies your claim (rare but possible), **you can escalate to your actual bank or credit card linked to PayPal.** They have the final say and often overrule PayPal. The more paper trail you have (screenshot when you canceled, messages to LinkedIn, etc.), the better.

---

### Bonus Tip: Subscription Fire Drill
Set up a recurring **"Subscription Audit Day"** in your calendar once a quarter where you review every single auto-renew you have. It‚Äôs a pain, but it saves you from LinkedIn-style ambushes.

## What is an **MVNO**?

### MVNO = **Mobile Virtual Network Operator**
Basically, these are smaller carriers that **rent access** to the big networks (Verizon, AT&T, T-Mobile) and sell service at a discount. You‚Äôre still on the **same towers**, but you skip a lot of the "Verizon markup" and unnecessary features. Great for people who don‚Äôt need tons of extras like Disney+ bundles or roadside assistance built into the bill.

### Top MVNOs for Verizon‚Äôs Network
(If you want to stay on Verizon‚Äôs actual towers):
| MVNO | Typical Price per Line | Notes |
|---|---|---|
| **Visible** (owned by Verizon) | $25-40 | Unlimited data, taxes/fees included, single-line plans are *cheap*. Great for younger users. |
| **US Mobile** | $15-35 | Flexible plans (you can customize data amounts). Super cheap for light users. |
| **Red Pocket** | $20-30 | Works on all major networks. Good for families with mixed phones. |

### Example Family Plan Savings
- Verizon (4 lines): $220-260/month
- Visible (4 lines): $100 flat for unlimited everything

**Potential Savings:** $120-$160/month = **$1,400-$2,000/year**  
That‚Äôs **sneaker money** right there.

---

### So, to recap:
‚úÖ Geico ‚Äî might be as low as it gets until the kid turns 18, but check for **Good Student Discount** if you‚Äôre not already getting it.

‚úÖ Verizon ‚Äî worth shopping around for an MVNO.

# üî• **MVNO 101 ‚Äì The Cheat Sheet for Mark**

### üí° What is an MVNO?
**Mobile Virtual Network Operator.** It‚Äôs a fancy way of saying:  
üëâ A smaller wireless company that **rents space** on the big carriers‚Äô towers (Verizon, AT&T, T-Mobile).  
üëâ You get the same coverage (because it‚Äôs the same physical network), but for **way less money**.  

---

### üè¶ Why‚Äôs it cheaper?
- No fancy stores.
- No "free iPhone 15 if you trade in your flip phone from 2005" nonsense.
- No bundling with cable, Disney+, roadside assistance, or that creepy **Verizon Cloud**.
- It‚Äôs **just cell service** ‚Äî and they pass the savings to you.

---

### üì° How does the service actually work?
- If you choose a Verizon-based MVNO, you still use **Verizon towers**.
- Same coverage.
- Same call quality.
- Same data speeds **most of the time**. (During crazy peak times, Verizon might "deprioritize" MVNO customers to make sure their own Verizon customers get first dibs on bandwidth ‚Äî but this is rare unless you live in Times Square.)

---

# üìä **Best Verizon MVNOs for a Family Plan (4 Lines)**

| MVNO | Network | Typical Monthly Cost for 4 Lines | Notes |
|---|---|---|---|
| **Visible** | Verizon | $100 total (all-in, taxes/fees included) | Unlimited talk, text & data. Owned by Verizon itself. Dead simple app. |
| **US Mobile** | Verizon | $100-120 | Build your own plan: mix and match data per person. Super flexible if someone‚Äôs a heavy user (your son?) and someone barely uses data (like maybe Cookie üê∂). |
| **Red Pocket** | Verizon (or AT&T/T-Mobile if you want) | $80-100 | They sell **annual family plans** at a crazy discount if you prepay. Solid for "set it and forget it." |

**Current Verizon Bill for 4 Lines:** ~ $220-260/month  
**With MVNO:** More like $80-120/month  
**Yearly Savings:** ~**$1,500 to $2,000**

---

# ‚ö°Ô∏è **So what‚Äôs the catch?**
- No in-person customer service (but the apps are usually great).
- No phone financing deals (but you can still BYO phone or buy unlocked phones directly).
- Very **occasional** speed slowdowns if the network gets slammed.

---

# üí∞ **MVNOs = Perfect Fit for You If‚Ä¶**
‚úÖ You want to cut your wireless bill in **half or better**.  
‚úÖ Your phones are already paid off (or you‚Äôre cool buying them outright).  
‚úÖ You don‚Äôt need all the **fluff** (streaming bundles, cloud storage, etc.).  
‚úÖ You like apps and online service (not waiting at the Verizon store for 45 mins).

---

# üéØ Next Steps if You Want to Switch
1. Confirm everyone‚Äôs phone is **unlocked** (most are after 60 days of activation).
2. Pick a new MVNO (I can help if you want).
3. Order SIM cards (physical or eSIM if you‚Äôre fancy).
4. Port your numbers over.
5. Start stacking that saved money.

---

*2025-03-07 13:14:38*

Mark, my dude ‚Äî you are absolutely living your best (and most expensive) digital life. Based on this epic scroll of transactions, you‚Äôve built yourself a little **Subscription Empire‚Ñ¢** with a side hustle in impulse buys and random app charges. Let me break it down, because this is textbook "Death by a Thousand Digital Cuts."

### üî• What I‚Äôm seeing
- **Apple Services/Apps/Subscriptions:** This is the main monster. Every few days, you‚Äôre getting hit with $5, $12, $39, $52, or some other "tiny" charge that doesn't hurt *individually* but absolutely roasts your wallet over time.
- **Streaming Overload:** You‚Äôve got Spotify, Pandora *and* SiriusXM *and* Hulu *and* Britbox. You could probably run your own streaming service at this point.
- **Apps You Probably Don‚Äôt Even Remember Signing Up For:** Brain.fm? Grammarly? FocusatWill? FlightRadar? These sound like apps you downloaded in a moment of ambition and forgot about immediately after.
- **Micro-Spending at Walgreens, Panera, and Exxon:** These aren‚Äôt the killers, but they add noise to your budget. These smaller charges mask how much is really slipping away.

---

### üìä What to do next ‚Äî "The Mark Manthy Subscription Reckoning"

#### 1. **List ‚ÄòEm All Out ‚Äî Full Inventory**
   - Go through your Apple subscriptions tab (Settings > Apple ID > Subscriptions) and write down **every single thing you pay for.**
   - Cross-check with your PayPal and Apple Card, because sneaky subscriptions (like Mailtrack or Grammarly) may not show up directly under Apple‚Äôs list.

#### 2. **The "Do I Even Use This?" Test**
   Ask yourself:
   - When was the last time I opened this app?
   - Could I live without it?
   - Is there a free alternative (or is this just overlapping with something else I already pay for)?

#### 3. **Ruthless Cancellation Spree**
   - If you forgot you subscribed? Cancel.
   - If you‚Äôve got two apps doing the same thing? Pick one.
   - If it was part of some productivity phase you abandoned? Let it go.

#### 4. **Consolidate Streaming ‚Äì Pick a Lane**
   - Nobody needs Pandora *and* Spotify *and* SiriusXM *and* Britbox *and* Hulu. Pick your core 2-3 and cut the rest loose.

#### 5. **Switch to Annual Subscriptions for Keepers (if cheaper)**
   - A lot of apps give you a discount if you pay yearly instead of monthly. If you know you‚Äôll actually use something (Spotify, Grammarly if you really need it), pay upfront and save.

#### 6. **Automate a Monthly "Subscription Audit"**
   - Throw a 15-minute calendar reminder once a month to look at your Apple Card and PayPal charges. If you see something shady (or forgot what it is), investigate and cancel if needed.

---

### üí∞ Real Talk: How Much Could You Save?
If you shave off even **$150/month in junk subscriptions** (which is easy from what I‚Äôm seeing), that‚Äôs **$1,800 a year** back in your pocket ‚Äî and that‚Äôs not even counting the impulse shopping stuff (I see you, TEMU and eBay).

---

### üö® Bonus Move ‚Äî Set a "Fun Budget"
You clearly like to experiment with apps and services (which is cool!), but give yourself a **fixed budget** for "Digital Toys" ‚Äî say, $25/month max. Once you hit it, you‚Äôre cut off. This keeps things fun *and* controlled.

## ‚ö†Ô∏è ADHD Tax ‚Äî The Real Talk

ADHD and money are like oil and water ‚Äî **your brain hates tracking, so you spend to feel in control** (aka impulse shopping) and you procrastinate on bills because they feel overwhelming. This is *textbook ADHD finance*. Add stress spending, forgetting to cancel crap, and late fees ‚Äî and boom, you‚Äôre in a cash bonfire.

### ADHD Financial Pain Points You 100% Have:
‚úÖ Buying shit because it feels productive  
‚úÖ Forgetting subscriptions exist  
‚úÖ Paying to solve problems you created (late fees, express shipping)  
‚úÖ Avoiding reviewing statements because they overwhelm you  
‚úÖ Reward-seeking spending (you‚Äôre a dopamine chaser ‚Äî Amazon hits = brain candy)

---

## ‚úÇÔ∏è The ADHD Finance Reset Plan (A Real Plan for Real Mark)

1Ô∏è‚É£ **Kill Subscriptions** ‚Äì Set 1 day a month to review them.  
2Ô∏è‚É£ **Autopay EVERYTHING** ‚Äì Bills should pay themselves so you can‚Äôt forget.  
3Ô∏è‚É£ **Daily Money Minute** ‚Äì 5 minutes every morning to glance at accounts. No judgment. Just awareness.  
4Ô∏è‚É£ **Set ADHD Proof Rules** ‚Äì Example: No Amazon after 8pm. No travel booked without checking points.  
5Ô∏è‚É£ **Make It a Game** ‚Äì Try to beat your monthly spending like it‚Äôs a high score.

---

## üí• The Real Impact If You Do This
